</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Meat</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Meat_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Meat (disambiguation)">Meat (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FoodMeat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/FoodMeat.jpg/220px-FoodMeat.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/FoodMeat.jpg/330px-FoodMeat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/FoodMeat.jpg/440px-FoodMeat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2888" data-file-height="1916" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FoodMeat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A selection of uncooked red meat and poultry</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Meat</b> is animal <a href="/wiki/Flesh" title="Flesh">flesh</a> that is eaten as <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1</sup> Humans have hunted and killed animals for meat since prehistoric times. The advent of civilization allowed the domestication of animals such as chickens, sheep, pigs and cattle. This eventually led to their use in meat production on an industrial scale with the aid of <a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouses" class="mw-redirect" title="Slaughterhouses">slaughterhouses</a>.</p>
<p>Meat is mainly composed of water, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>. It is edible raw, but is normally eaten after it has been cooked and seasoned or processed in a variety of ways. Unprocessed meat will <a href="/wiki/Meat_spoilage" title="Meat spoilage">spoil or rot</a> within hours or days as a result of infection with and decomposition by <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fungi" class="mw-redirect" title="Fungi">fungi</a>.</p>
<p>Most often, <i>meat</i> refers to <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscle</a> and associated <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> and other tissues, but it may also describe other edible tissues such as <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">offal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1</sup> <i>Meat</i> is sometimes also used in a more restrictive sense – the flesh of <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammalian</a> species (pigs, cattle, lambs, etc.) raised and prepared for human consumption, to the exclusion of <a href="/wiki/Fish_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fish (food)">fish</a>, other <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> or other animals.<sup id="cite_ref-Meat1_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meat1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Meat2_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meat2-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word <i>meat</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> word <i>mete</i>, which referred to food in general. The term is related to <i>mad</i> in <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a>, <i>mat</i> in <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>, and <i>matur</i> in <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>, which also mean 'food'. The word <i>mete</i> also exists in <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a> (and to a lesser extent, modern <a href="/wiki/West_Frisian_language" title="West Frisian language">West Frisian</a>) to denote important food, differentiating it from <i>swiets</i> (sweets) and <i>dierfied</i> (animal feed).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">History of agriculture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">Paleontological</a> evidence suggests that meat constituted a substantial proportion of the diet of even the earliest humans.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup> Early <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> depended on the organized hunting of large animals such as <a href="/wiki/Bison" title="Bison">bison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> of animals, of which we have evidence dating back to the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a> (c. 10,000 BC),<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup> allowed the systematic production of meat and the <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">breeding</a> of animals with a view to improving meat production.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup> The animals which are now the principal sources of meat were domesticated in conjunction with the development of early civilizations:</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lamb_meat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lamb_meat.jpg/220px-Lamb_meat.jpg" width="220" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lamb_meat.jpg/330px-Lamb_meat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lamb_meat.jpg/440px-Lamb_meat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1019" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lamb_meat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A typical shoulder cut of <a href="/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton" title="Lamb and mutton">lamb</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">Sheep</a>, originating from western Asia, were domesticated with the help of dogs prior to the establishment of settled <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, likely as early as the 8th millennium BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:3</sup> Several breeds of sheep were established in ancient <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> by 3500–3000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:3</sup> Today, more than 200 <a href="/wiki/List_of_sheep_breeds" title="List of sheep breeds">sheep-breeds</a> exist.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> were domesticated in Mesopotamia after settled agriculture was established about 5000 BC,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5</sup> and several breeds were established by 2500 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:6</sup> Modern domesticated cattle fall into the groups <i><a href="/wiki/Bos_taurus" class="mw-redirect" title="Bos taurus">Bos taurus</a></i> (European cattle) and <i><a href="/wiki/Bos_indicus" class="mw-redirect" title="Bos indicus">Bos indicus</a></i> (zebu), both descended from the now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5</sup> The breeding of <a href="/wiki/Beef_cattle" title="Beef cattle">beef cattle</a>, cattle optimized for meat production as opposed to animals best suited for draught or dairy purposes, began in the middle of the 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:7</sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hereford_bull_large.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/220px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/330px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/440px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="369" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hereford_bull_large.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Hereford_(cattle)" title="Hereford (cattle)">Hereford</a> bull, a breed of cattle frequently used in beef production.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Domestic_pig" title="Domestic pig">Domestic pigs</a>, which are descended from <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boars</a>, are known to have existed about 2500 BC in modern-day Hungary and in <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a>; earlier pottery from <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a> and Egypt depicts wild pigs.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8</sup> <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">Pork</a> sausages and <a href="/wiki/Ham" title="Ham">hams</a> were of great commercial importance in <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman" class="mw-redirect" title="Greco-Roman">Greco-Roman</a> times.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:8</sup> Pigs continue to be bred intensively as they are being optimized to produce meat best suited for specific meat products.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:9</sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Other animals are or have been raised or hunted for their flesh. The type of meat consumed varies much between different cultures, changes over time, depending on factors such as tradition and the availability of the animals. The amount and kind of meat consumed also varies by income, both between countries and within a given country.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Horse_meat" title="Horse meat">Horses</a> are commonly eaten in France,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Italy, Germany and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, among other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Horses and other large <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a> were hunted during the late <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> in western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dog_meat" title="Dog meat">Dogs</a> are consumed in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Dogs are also occasionally eaten in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> regions.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Historically, dog meat has been consumed in various part of the world, such as <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Japan,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cat_meat" title="Cat meat">Cats</a> are consumed in Southern China, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and sometimes also in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Italy" title="Northern Italy">Northern Italy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Guinea_pig" title="Guinea pig">Guinea pigs</a> are raised for their flesh in the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">Whales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> are hunted, partly for their flesh, in Japan, <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, Canada, the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Vincent_and_the_Grenadines" title="Saint Vincent and the Grenadines">Saint Vincent and the Grenadines</a> and by two small communities in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Modern agriculture employs a number of techniques, such as <a href="/wiki/Progeny_testing" title="Progeny testing">progeny testing</a>, to speed <a href="/wiki/Artificial_selection" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial selection">artificial selection</a> by breeding animals to rapidly acquire the qualities desired by meat producers.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:10</sup> For instance, in the wake of well-publicised health concerns associated with <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fats</a> in the 1980s, the fat content of United Kingdom beef, pork and lamb fell from 20–26 percent to 4–8 percent within a few decades, due to both selective breeding for leanness and changed methods of <a href="/wiki/Butchery" class="mw-redirect" title="Butchery">butchery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:10</sup> Methods of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> aimed at improving the meat production qualities of animals are now also becoming available.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:14</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg/220px-SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg/330px-SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg/440px-SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fresh meat in a <a href="/wiki/Supermarket" title="Supermarket">supermarket</a> in North America</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Even though it is a very old industry, meat production continues to be shaped strongly by the evolving demands of customers. The trend towards selling meat in pre-packaged cuts has increased the demand for larger breeds of cattle, which are better suited to producing such cuts.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup> Even more animals not previously exploited for their meat are now being farmed, especially the more agile and mobile species, whose muscles tend to be developed better than those of cattle, sheep or pigs.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup> Examples are the various <a href="/wiki/Antelope" title="Antelope">antelope</a> species, the <a href="/wiki/Zebra" title="Zebra">zebra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11ff</sup> as well as non-mammals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">ostrich</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:13</sup> Another important trend in contemporary meat production is <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic farming</a> which, while providing no <a href="/wiki/Organoleptic" title="Organoleptic">organoleptic</a> benefit to meat so produced,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> meets an increasing demand for organic meat.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption">Consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Meat consumption varies worldwide, depending on cultural or religious preferences, as well as economic conditions. <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">Vegetarians</a> choose not to eat meat because of ethical, economic, environmental, religious or health concerns that are associated with meat production and consumption.</p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Meat_Atlas_2014_--_Meat_Consumption_in_industrialised_countries.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Meat_Atlas_2014_--_Meat_Consumption_in_industrialised_countries.png/300px-Meat_Atlas_2014_--_Meat_Consumption_in_industrialised_countries.png" width="300" height="206" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Meat_Atlas_2014_--_Meat_Consumption_in_industrialised_countries.png/450px-Meat_Atlas_2014_--_Meat_Consumption_in_industrialised_countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Meat_Atlas_2014_--_Meat_Consumption_in_industrialised_countries.png/600px-Meat_Atlas_2014_--_Meat_Consumption_in_industrialised_countries.png 2x" data-file-width="1493" data-file-height="1027" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">While meat consumption in most industrialized countries is at high, stable levels...<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Meat_Atlas_2014_meat_consumption_developing_countries.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Meat_Atlas_2014_meat_consumption_developing_countries.png/300px-Meat_Atlas_2014_meat_consumption_developing_countries.png" width="300" height="206" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Meat_Atlas_2014_meat_consumption_developing_countries.png/450px-Meat_Atlas_2014_meat_consumption_developing_countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Meat_Atlas_2014_meat_consumption_developing_countries.png/600px-Meat_Atlas_2014_meat_consumption_developing_countries.png 2x" data-file-width="1493" data-file-height="1027" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">... meat consumption in emerging economies is on the rise.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the analysis of the FAO the overall consumption for <a href="#Red_and_white_meat">white meat</a> between 1990 and 2009 has dramatically increased. For example, poultry meat has increased by 76.6% per kilo per capita and pig meat by 19.7%. However, on the contrary, bovine meat has decreased from 10.4 kilograms (23&#160;lb)/capita in 1990 to 9.6 kilograms (21&#160;lb)/capita in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_meat_consumption" title="List of countries by meat consumption">List of countries by meat consumption</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_and_development_of_meat_animals">Growth and development of meat animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Growth and development of meat animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agricultural_science" title="Agricultural science">Agricultural science</a> has identified several factors bearing on the growth and development of meat in animals.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin:10px">
<tr>
<th>Trait</th>
<th>Heritability<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Reproductive efficiency</td>
<td>2–10%</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Meat quality</td>
<td>15–30%</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Growth</td>
<td>20–40%</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Muscle/fat ratio</td>
<td>40–60%</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Several economically important traits in meat animals are heritable to some degree (see the adjacent table) and can thus be selected for by <a href="/wiki/Animal_breeding" title="Animal breeding">animal breeding</a>. In cattle, certain growth features are controlled by <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(genetics)" title="Dominance (genetics)">recessive genes</a> which have not so far been controlled, complicating breeding.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:18</sup> One such trait is <a href="/wiki/Dwarfism" title="Dwarfism">dwarfism</a>; another is the doppelender or "<a href="/wiki/Myostatin" title="Myostatin">double muscling</a>" condition, which causes <a href="/wiki/Muscle_hypertrophy" title="Muscle hypertrophy">muscle hypertrophy</a> and thereby increases the animal's commercial value.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:18</sup> <a href="/wiki/Genetic_analysis" title="Genetic analysis">Genetic analysis</a> continues to reveal the genetic mechanisms that control numerous aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">endocrine system</a> and, through it, meat growth and quality.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:19</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">Genetic engineering</a> techniques can shorten breeding programmes significantly because they allow for the identification and isolation of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> coding for desired traits, and for the reincorporation of these genes into the animal <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:21</sup> To enable such manipulation, research is ongoing (as of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>) to <a href="/wiki/Genome_project" title="Genome project">map the entire genome</a> of sheep, cattle and pigs.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:21</sup> Some research has already seen commercial application. For instance, a <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombinant</a> <a href="/wiki/Bacterium" class="mw-redirect" title="Bacterium">bacterium</a> has been developed which improves the digestion of grass in the <a href="/wiki/Rumen" title="Rumen">rumen</a> of cattle, and some specific features of muscle fibres have been genetically altered.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:22</sup></p>
<p>Experimental <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_cloning" class="mw-redirect" title="Reproductive cloning">reproductive cloning</a> of commercially important meat animals such as sheep, pig or cattle has been successful. The multiple asexual reproduction of animals bearing desirable traits can thus be anticipated,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:22</sup> although this is not yet practical on a commercial scale.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Heat regulation in <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> is of great economic significance, because mammals attempt to maintain a constant optimal body temperature. Low temperatures tend to prolong animal development and high temperatures tend to retard it.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:22</sup> Depending on their size, body shape and insulation through tissue and fur, some animals have a relatively narrow zone of temperature tolerance and others (e.g. cattle) a broad one.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:23</sup> Static <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>, for reasons still unknown, also retard animal development.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:23</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Nutrition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The quality and quantity of usable meat depends on the animal's <i>plane of nutrition</i>, i.e., whether it is over- or underfed. Scientists disagree, however, about how exactly the plane of nutrition influences carcase composition.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:25</sup></p>
<p>The composition of the diet, especially the amount of protein provided, is also an important factor regulating animal growth.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:26</sup> <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">Ruminants</a>, which may digest <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, are better adapted to poor-quality diets, but their ruminal microorganisms degrade high-quality protein if supplied in excess.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:27</sup> Because producing high-quality protein animal feed is expensive (see also <i><a href="/wiki/Meat#Environmental_impact" title="Meat">Environmental impact</a></i> below), several techniques are employed or experimented with to ensure maximum utilization of protein. These include the treatment of feed with <a href="/wiki/Formalin" class="mw-redirect" title="Formalin">formalin</a> to protect <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> during their passage through the <a href="/wiki/Rumen" title="Rumen">rumen</a>, the recycling of <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a> by feeding it back to cattle mixed with feed concentrates, or the partial conversion of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> to protein through microbial action.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:30</sup></p>
<p>In plant feed, environmental factors influence the availability of crucial <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> or <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a>, a lack or excess of which can cause a great many ailments.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:29</sup> In Australia, for instance, where the soil contains limited <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a>, cattle are being fed additional phosphate to increase the efficiency of beef production.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:28</sup> Also in Australia, cattle and sheep in certain areas were often found losing their appetite and dying in the midst of rich pasture; this was at length found to be a result of <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> deficiency in the soil.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:29</sup> Plant <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a> are also a risk to grazing animals; for instance, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoracetate" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodium fluoracetate">sodium fluoracetate</a>, found in some African and Australian plants, kills by disrupting the <a href="/wiki/Cellular_metabolism" class="mw-redirect" title="Cellular metabolism">cellular metabolism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:29</sup> Certain man-made <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutants</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Methylmercury" title="Methylmercury">methylmercury</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a> residues present a particular hazard due to their tendency to <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulate" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioaccumulate">bioaccumulate</a> in meat, potentially poisoning consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:30</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_intervention">Human intervention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Human intervention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Meat producers may seek to improve the <a href="/wiki/Fertility" title="Fertility">fertility</a> of female animals through the administration of <a href="/wiki/Gonadotrophic" class="mw-redirect" title="Gonadotrophic">gonadotrophic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ovulation" title="Ovulation">ovulation</a>-inducing <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:31</sup> In pig production, <a href="/wiki/Domestic_pig" title="Domestic pig">sow</a> infertility is a common problem — possibly due to excessive fatness.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:32</sup> No methods currently exist to augment the fertility of male animals.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:32</sup> <a href="/wiki/Artificial_insemination" title="Artificial insemination">Artificial insemination</a> is now routinely used to produce animals of the best possible genetic quality, and the efficiency of this method is improved through the administration of hormones that synchronize the ovulation cycles within groups of females.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:33</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">Growth hormones</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Anabolic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabolic">anabolic</a> agents such as <a href="/wiki/Steroid" title="Steroid">steroids</a>, are used in some countries to accelerate muscle growth in animals.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:33</sup> This practice has given rise to the <a href="/wiki/Beef_hormone_controversy" title="Beef hormone controversy">beef hormone controversy</a>, an international trade dispute. It may also decrease the tenderness of meat, although research on this is inconclusive,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:35</sup> and have other effects on the composition of the muscle flesh.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:36ff</sup> Where <a href="/wiki/Castration" title="Castration">castration</a> is used to improve control over male animals, its side effects are also counteracted by the administration of hormones.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:33</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sedative" title="Sedative">Sedatives</a> may be administered to animals to counteract stress factors and increase weight gain.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:39</sup> The feeding of <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> to certain animals has been shown to improve growth rates also.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:39</sup> This practice is particularly prevalent in the USA, but has been banned in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a>, partly because it causes <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogenic</a> microorganisms.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:39</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biochemical_composition">Biochemical composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Biochemical composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Numerous aspects of the biochemical composition of meat vary in complex ways depending on the species, breed, sex, age, plane of nutrition, training and exercise of the animal, as well as on the anatomical location of the musculature involved.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:94–126</sup> Even between animals of the same litter and sex there are considerable differences in such parameters as the percentage of intramuscular fat.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:126</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_constituents">Main constituents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Main constituents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adult mammalian <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> flesh consists of roughly 75 percent water, 19 percent protein, 2.5 percent intramuscular fat, 1.2 percent <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and 2.3 percent other soluble non-protein substances. These include <a href="/wiki/Nitrogenous" class="mw-redirect" title="Nitrogenous">nitrogenous</a> compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, and inorganic substances such as minerals.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:76</sup></p>
<p>Muscle proteins are either soluble in water (<a href="/wiki/Sarcoplasm" title="Sarcoplasm">sarcoplasmic</a> proteins, about 11.5 percent of total muscle mass) or in concentrated salt solutions (<a href="/wiki/Myofibrillar" class="mw-redirect" title="Myofibrillar">myofibrillar</a> proteins, about 5.5 percent of mass).<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:75</sup> There are several hundred sarcoplasmic proteins.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:77</sup> Most of them – the glycolytic <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> – are involved in the <a href="/wiki/Glycolytic_pathway" class="mw-redirect" title="Glycolytic pathway">glycolytic pathway</a>, i.e., the conversion of stored energy into muscle power.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:78</sup> The two most abundant myofibrillar proteins, <a href="/wiki/Myosin" title="Myosin">myosin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:79</sup> are responsible for the muscle's overall structure. The remaining protein mass consists of <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a> (<a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elastin" title="Elastin">elastin</a>) as well as <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelle</a> tissue.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:79</sup></p>
<p>Fat in meat can be either <a href="/wiki/Adipose_tissue" title="Adipose tissue">adipose tissue</a>, used by the animal to store energy and consisting of "true fats" (<a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a> of <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> with <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:82</sup> or intramuscular fat, which contains considerable quantities of <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Saponification" title="Saponification">unsaponifiable</a> constituents such as <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:82</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Red_and_white_meat">Red and white meat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Red and white meat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Blade_steak.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Blade_steak.jpg/220px-Blade_steak.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Blade_steak.jpg/330px-Blade_steak.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Blade_steak.jpg/440px-Blade_steak.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Blade_steak.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Blade steaks are an example of "red" meat</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Meat can be broadly classified as "red" or "white" depending on the concentration of <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> in muscle fibre. When myoglobin is exposed to <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, reddish oxymyoglobin develops, making myoglobin-rich meat appear red. The redness of meat depends on species, animal age, and fibre type: <a href="/wiki/Red_meat" title="Red meat">Red meat</a> contains more narrow muscle fibres that tend to operate over long periods without rest,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:93</sup> while <a href="/wiki/White_meat" title="White meat">white meat</a> contains more broad fibres that tend to work in short fast bursts.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:93</sup></p>
<p>Generally, the meat of adult mammals such as <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">cows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutton" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutton">sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goat_meat" title="Goat meat">goats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Horse_meat" title="Horse meat">horses</a> is considered red, while <a href="/wiki/Chicken_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicken (food)">chicken</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Turkey (food)">turkey</a> breast meat is considered white.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutritional_information">Nutritional information</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Nutritional information">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-left:1em;">

<td style="background:#fff; text-align:center;">0&#160;g</td>
