</table>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> tissue is very high in protein, containing all of the <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a>, and in most cases is a good source of <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">vitamin B<sub>12</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niacin" title="Niacin">niacin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B6" title="Vitamin B6">vitamin B<sub>6</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Choline" title="Choline">choline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riboflavin" title="Riboflavin">riboflavin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-beef.org_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beef.org-27">[27]</a></sup> Several forms of meat are also high in <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">vitamin K</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-k2_foods_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k2_foods-28">[28]</a></sup> Muscle tissue is very low in carbohydrates and does not contain dietary fiber.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> While taste quality may vary between meats, the proteins, vitamins, and minerals available from meats are generally consistent.</p>
<p>The fat content of meat can vary widely depending on the <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> and <a href="/wiki/Breed" title="Breed">breed</a> of animal, the way in which the animal was raised, including what it was fed, the <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomical</a> part of the body, and the methods of butchering and cooking. Wild animals such as <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a> are typically leaner than farm animals, leading those concerned about fat content to choose <a href="/wiki/Game_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Game (food)">game</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Venison" title="Venison">venison</a>. Decades of breeding meat animals for fatness is being reversed by consumer demand for meat with less fat. The fatty deposits that exist with the muscle fibers in meats soften meat when it is cooked and improve the flavor through chemical changes initiated through heat that allow the protein and fat molecules to interact. The fat, when cooked with meat, also makes the meat seem juicier. However, the nutritional contribution of the fat is mainly calories as opposed to protein. As fat content rises, the meat's contribution to nutrition declines. In addition, there is <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> associated with fat surrounding the meat. The cholesterol is a lipid associated with the kind of saturated fat found in meat. The increase in meat consumption after 1960 is associated with, though not definitively the cause of, significant imbalances of fat and cholesterol in the human diet.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The table in this section compares the nutritional content of several types of meat. While each kind of meat has about the same content of protein and carbohydrates, there is a very wide range of fat content.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Meat_industry" title="Meat industry">Meat industry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meat_packing_industry" title="Meat packing industry">Meat packing industry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal_slaughter" title="Animal slaughter">Animal slaughter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouse" title="Slaughterhouse">Slaughterhouse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Butchery" class="mw-redirect" title="Butchery">Butchery</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry" title="Fishing industry">Fishing industry</a></div>
<p>Meat is produced by killing an animal and cutting flesh out of it. These procedures are called <a href="/wiki/Animal_slaughter" title="Animal slaughter">slaughter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butchery" class="mw-redirect" title="Butchery">butchery</a>, respectively. There is ongoing research into producing <a href="/wiki/In_vitro_meat" class="mw-redirect" title="In vitro meat">meat <i>in vitro</i></a>, that is, outside of animals.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Upon reaching a predetermined age or weight, livestock are usually transported <i>en masse</i> to the slaughterhouse. Depending on its length and circumstances, this may exert stress and injuries on the animals, and some may die <i>en route</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:129</sup> Unnecessary stress in transport may adversely affect the quality of the meat.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:129</sup> In particular, the muscles of stressed animals are low in water and <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>, and their <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> fails to attain acidic values, all of which results in poor meat quality.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:130</sup> Consequently, and also due to campaigning by <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a> groups, laws and industry practices in several countries tend to become more restrictive with respect to the duration and other circumstances of livestock transports.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slaughter">Slaughter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Slaughter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Animals are usually slaughtered by being first <a href="/wiki/Stunned" class="mw-redirect" title="Stunned">stunned</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Exsanguination" title="Exsanguination">exsanguinated</a> (bled out). Death results from the one or the other procedure, depending on the methods employed. Stunning can be effected through <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxiating</a> the animals with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, shooting them with a <a href="/wiki/Gun" title="Gun">gun</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Captive_bolt_pistol" title="Captive bolt pistol">captive bolt pistol</a>, or shocking them with electric current.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:134ff</sup> In most forms of <a href="/wiki/Ritual_slaughter" title="Ritual slaughter">ritual slaughter</a>, stunning is not allowed.</p>
<p>Draining as much blood as possible from the carcass is necessary because blood causes the meat to have an unappealing appearance and is a breeding ground for microorganisms.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1340</sup> The exsanguination is accomplished by severing the <a href="/wiki/Carotid_artery" title="Carotid artery">carotid artery</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jugular_vein" title="Jugular vein">jugular vein</a> in cattle and sheep, and the <a href="/wiki/Anterior_vena_cava" class="mw-redirect" title="Anterior vena cava">anterior vena cava</a> in pigs.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:137</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dressing_and_cutting">Dressing and cutting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Dressing and cutting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After exsanguination, the carcass is dressed; that is, the head, feet, hide (except hogs and some veal), excess fat, <a href="/wiki/Viscera" class="mw-redirect" title="Viscera">viscera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">offal</a> are removed, leaving only bones and edible muscle.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:138</sup> Cattle and pig carcases, but not those of sheep, are then split in half along the mid ventral axis, and the carcase is cut into wholesale pieces.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:138</sup> The dressing and cutting sequence, long a province of manual labor, is progressively being fully automated.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:138</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conditioning">Conditioning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Conditioning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg/220px-MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg/330px-MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg/440px-MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4201" data-file-height="2866" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In the meat products sector of the <a href="/wiki/March%C3%A9_d%27Int%C3%A9r%C3%AAt_National_de_Rungis" class="mw-redirect" title="Marché d'Intérêt National de Rungis">Rungis International Market</a>, France.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under hygienic conditions and without other treatment, meat can be stored at above its freezing point (–1.5&#160;°C) for about six weeks without spoilage, during which time it undergoes an aging process that increases its tenderness and flavor.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:141</sup></p>
<p>During the first day after death, <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a> continues until the accumulation of <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a> causes the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> to reach about 5.5. The remaining <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>, about 18&#160;g per kg, is believed to increase the water-holding capacity and tenderness of the flesh when cooked.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:87</sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rigor_mortis" title="Rigor mortis">Rigor mortis</a></i> sets in a few hours after death as <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a> is used up, causing <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myosin" title="Myosin">myosin</a> to combine into rigid <a href="/wiki/Actomyosin" class="mw-redirect" title="Actomyosin">actomyosin</a> and lowering the meat's water-holding capacity,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:90</sup> causing it to lose water ("weep").<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:146</sup> In muscles that enter <i>rigor</i> in a contracted position, actin and myosin filaments overlap and cross-bond, resulting in meat that is tough on cooking<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:144</sup> – hence again the need to prevent pre-slaughter stress in the animal.</p>
<p>Over time, the muscle proteins <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denature</a> in varying degree, with the exception of the collagen and <a href="/wiki/Elastin" title="Elastin">elastin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:142</sup> and <i>rigor mortis</i> resolves. Because of these changes, the meat is tender and pliable when cooked just after death or after the resolution of <i>rigor</i>, but tough when cooked during <i>rigor.</i><sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:142</sup> As the muscle pigment <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> denatures, its iron <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidates</a>, which may cause a brown discoloration near the surface of the meat.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:146</sup> Ongoing <a href="/wiki/Proteolysis" title="Proteolysis">proteolysis</a> also contributes to conditioning. <a href="/wiki/Hypoxanthine" title="Hypoxanthine">Hypoxanthine</a>, a breakdown product of ATP, contributes to the meat's flavor and odor, as do other products of the decomposition of muscle fat and protein.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:155</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additives">Additives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Additives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sucuk-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sucuk-1.jpg/220px-Sucuk-1.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sucuk-1.jpg/330px-Sucuk-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sucuk-1.jpg/440px-Sucuk-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1824" data-file-height="1386" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sucuk-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The word "<a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausage</a>" is derived from <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>saussiche</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <i>salsus</i> meaning "salted".<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When meat is industrially processed in preparation of consumption, it may be enriched with <a href="/wiki/Food_additive" title="Food additive">additives</a> to protect or modify its flavor or color, to improve its tenderness, juiciness or cohesiveness, or to aid with its <a href="/wiki/Meat_preservation" class="mw-redirect" title="Meat preservation">preservation</a>. Meat additives include the following:<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">Salt</a> is the most frequently used additive in meat processing. It imparts flavor but also inhibits microbial growth, extends the product's shelf life and helps <a href="/wiki/Emulsifier" class="mw-redirect" title="Emulsifier">emulsifying</a> finely processed products, such as sausages. Ready-to-eat meat products normally contain about 1.5 to 2.5 percent salt.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup> Salt water or similar substances may also be injected into <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> meat to improve the taste and increase the weight, in a process called <a href="/wiki/Plumping" title="Plumping">plumping</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nitrite" title="Nitrite">Nitrite</a> is used in <a href="/wiki/Curing_meat" class="mw-redirect" title="Curing meat">curing meat</a> to stabilize the meat's color and flavor, and inhibits the growth of spore-forming microorganisms such as <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium_botulinum" title="Clostridium botulinum">C. botulinum</a></i>. The use of nitrite's precursor <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> is now limited to a few products such as dry sausage, <a href="/wiki/Prosciutto" title="Prosciutto">prosciutto</a> or <a href="/wiki/Parma_ham" class="mw-redirect" title="Parma ham">parma ham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">Phosphates</a> used in meat processing are normally alkaline <a href="/wiki/Polyphosphate" title="Polyphosphate">polyphosphates</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_tripolyphosphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodium tripolyphosphate">sodium tripolyphosphate</a>. They are used to increase the water-binding and emulsifying ability of meat proteins, but also limit lipid oxidation and flavor loss, and reduce microbial growth.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Erythorbate" class="mw-redirect" title="Erythorbate">Erythorbate</a> or its equivalent <a href="/wiki/Ascorbic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascorbic acid">ascorbic acid</a> (vitamin C) is used to stabilize the color of cured meat.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sweeteners" class="mw-redirect" title="Sweeteners">Sweeteners</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Corn_syrup" title="Corn syrup">corn syrup</a> impart a sweet flavor, bind water and assist surface browning during cooking in the <a href="/wiki/Maillard_reaction" title="Maillard reaction">Maillard reaction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Seasoning" title="Seasoning">Seasonings</a> impart or modify flavor. They include <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">spices</a> or <a href="/wiki/Oleoresin" title="Oleoresin">oleoresins</a> extracted from them, <a href="/wiki/Herb" title="Herb">herbs</a>, vegetables and <a href="/wiki/Essential_oil" title="Essential oil">essential oils</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flavoring" class="mw-redirect" title="Flavoring">Flavorings</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Monosodium_glutamate" title="Monosodium glutamate">monosodium glutamate</a> impart or strengthen a particular flavor.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tenderizing" class="mw-redirect" title="Tenderizing">Tenderizers</a> break down <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagens</a> to make the meat more palatable for consumption. They include <a href="/wiki/Proteolytic_enzyme" class="mw-redirect" title="Proteolytic enzyme">proteolytic enzymes</a>, acids, salt and phosphate.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>Dedicated <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">antimicrobials</a> include <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_diacetate" title="Sodium diacetate">sodium diacetate</a>, acidified <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> or <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sulfate" title="Calcium sulfate">calcium sulfate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cetylpyridinium_chloride" title="Cetylpyridinium chloride">cetylpyridinium chloride</a>, activated <a href="/wiki/Lactoferrin" title="Lactoferrin">lactoferrin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_lactate" title="Sodium lactate">sodium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potassium_lactate" title="Potassium lactate">potassium lactate</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Bacteriocin" title="Bacteriocin">bacteriocins</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Nisin" title="Nisin">nisin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">Antioxidants</a> include a wide range of chemicals that limit <a href="/wiki/Lipid_oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Lipid oxidation">lipid oxidation</a>, which creates an undesirable "off flavor", in precooked meat products.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Acidifier" title="Acidifier">Acidifiers</a>, most often lactic or citric acid, can impart a tangy or tart flavor note, extend shelf-life, tenderize fresh meat or help with protein <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denaturation</a> and moisture release in dried meat. They substitute for the process of natural fermentation that acidifies some meat products such as hard <a href="/wiki/Salami" title="Salami">salami</a> or prosciutto.<sup id="cite_ref-Mills.2C_Additives_32-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills.2C_Additives-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Misidentification">Misidentification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Misidentification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the rise of complex <a href="/wiki/Supply_chains" class="mw-redirect" title="Supply chains">supply chains</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Cold_chain" title="Cold chain">cold chains</a>, in developed economies, the distance between the farmer or fisherman and customer has grown, increasing the possibility for intentional and unintentional misidentification of meat at various points in the supply chain.<sup id="cite_ref-WPFish_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WPFish-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, reports emerged across Europe that products labelled as containing beef <a href="/wiki/2013_meat_adulteration_scandal" class="mw-redirect" title="2013 meat adulteration scandal">actually contained horse meat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In February 2013 a study was published showing that about one-third of raw fish are misidentified across the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-WPFish_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WPFish-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imitation_meat">Imitation meat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Imitation meat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Various forms of <a href="/wiki/Imitation_meat" class="mw-redirect" title="Imitation meat">imitation meat</a> have been created for people who wish not to eat meat but still want to taste its flavor and texture. Meat imitates are typically some form of processed <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybean</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>), but they can also be based on <a href="/wiki/Wheat_gluten_(food)" title="Wheat gluten (food)">wheat gluten</a> or even fungi (<a href="/wiki/Quorn_(food_product)" class="mw-redirect" title="Quorn (food product)">quorn</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_meat_production" title="Environmental impact of meat production">Environmental impact of meat production</a></div>
<p>Various environmental effects are associated with meat production. Among these are greenhouse gas emissions, fossil energy use, water use, water quality changes, and effects on grazed ecosystems.</p>
<p>The livestock sector may be the largest source of water pollution (due to animal wastes, fertilizers, pesticides), and it contributes to emergence of antibiotic resistance. It accounts for over 8% of global human water use. It is by far the biggest cause of land use, as it accounts for nearly 40% of the global land surface.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> It is a significant driver of <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">biodiversity loss</a>, as it causes <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, land degradation, pollution, and <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> It is reportedly responsible for 14.5% of the world's anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Although, a revised study of the environmental impact of livestock states that they account for 51% of worldwide greenhouse gas emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> However, it is not only meat production that causes environmental damage, because according to production data compiled by the FAO, 74 percent of global livestock product tonnage in 2011 was accounted for by non-meat products.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The occurrence, nature and significance of environmental effects varies among livestock production systems.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Grazing of livestock can be beneficial for some wildlife species, but not for others.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Targeted grazing of livestock is used as a food-producing alternative to herbicide use in some vegetation management.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Meat-producing livestock can provide environmental benefits through waste reduction, e.g. conversion of human-inedible residues of food crops.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Manure from meat-producing livestock is used as fertilizer; it may be composted before application to food crops. Substitution of animal manures for synthetic fertilizers in crop production can be environmentally significant, as between 43 and 88 MJ of fossil fuel energy are used per kg of nitrogen in manufacture of synthetic nitrogenous fertilizers.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a report produced by <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a>'s (UNEP) international panel for sustainable resource management, a worldwide transition in the direction of a meat and dairy free diet is indispensable if adverse global climate change were to be prevented.<sup id="cite_ref-TG-20100602_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TG-20100602-50">[50]</a></sup> Meat consumption is considered one of the primary contributors of the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">sixth mass extinction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz2015_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz2015-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spoilage_and_preservation">Spoilage and preservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Spoilage and preservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Meat_spoilage" title="Meat spoilage">Meat spoilage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meat_preservation" class="mw-redirect" title="Meat preservation">Meat preservation</a></div>
<p>The spoilage of meat occurs, if untreated, in a matter of hours or days and results in the meat becoming unappetizing, poisonous or infectious. Spoilage is caused by the practically unavoidable infection and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decomposition</a> of meat by <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, which are borne by the animal itself, by the people handling the meat, and by their implements. Meat can be kept edible for a much longer time – though not indefinitely – if proper <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> is observed during production and processing, and if appropriate <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">food safety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">food preservation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_storage" title="Food storage">food storage</a> procedures are applied. Without the application of <a href="/wiki/Preservative" title="Preservative">preservatives</a> and stabilizers, the fats in meat may also begin to rapidly decompose after cooking or processing, leading to an objectionable taste known as <a href="/wiki/Warmed_over_flavor" class="mw-redirect" title="Warmed over flavor">warmed over flavor</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_of_preparation">Methods of preparation<span id="Processed_meat"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Methods of preparation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG/220px-Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG/330px-Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG/440px-Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A spit <a href="/wiki/Barbecue" title="Barbecue">barbecue</a> at a street fair in New York City's <a href="/wiki/East_Village,_Manhattan" title="East Village, Manhattan">East Village</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Fresh meat can be cooked for immediate consumption, or be processed, that is, treated for longer-term <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">preservation</a> and later consumption, possibly after further preparation. Fresh meat cuts or processed cuts may produce iridescence, commonly thought to be due to spoilage but actually caused structural coloration and <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> of the light.<sup id="cite_ref-foods_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foods-54">[54]</a></sup> A common additive to processed meats, both for preservation and because it prevents discoloring, is <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrite" title="Sodium nitrite">sodium nitrite</a>, which, however, is also a source of health concerns, because it may form <a href="/wiki/Carcinogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Carcinogenic">carcinogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Nitrosamine" title="Nitrosamine">nitrosamines</a> when heated.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meat is prepared in many ways, as <a href="/wiki/Steak" title="Steak">steaks</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Stew" title="Stew">stews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fondue" title="Fondue">fondue</a>, or as <a href="/wiki/Dried_meat" title="Dried meat">dried meat</a> like beef jerky. It may be ground then formed into patties (as <a href="/wiki/Hamburger" title="Hamburger">hamburgers</a> or croquettes), loaves, or <a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausages</a>, or used in loose form (as in "sloppy joe" or <a href="/wiki/Bolognese_sauce" title="Bolognese sauce">Bolognese sauce</a>).</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Porksmoke1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Porksmoke1.jpg/220px-Porksmoke1.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Porksmoke1.jpg/330px-Porksmoke1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Porksmoke1.jpg/440px-Porksmoke1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Porksmoke1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pork ribs being smoked</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some meat is cured by <a href="/wiki/Smoking_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Smoking (food)">smoking</a>, which is the process of <a href="/wiki/Seasoning" title="Seasoning">flavoring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cooking</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">preserving</a> <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> by exposing it to the <a href="/wiki/Smoke" title="Smoke">smoke</a> from burning or smoldering plant materials, most often <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alder" title="Alder">alder</a> is the traditional smoking wood, but <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> is more often used now, and <a href="/wiki/Beech" title="Beech">beech</a> to a lesser extent. In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hickory" title="Hickory">hickory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesquite" title="Mesquite">mesquite</a>, oak, <a href="/wiki/Pecan" title="Pecan">pecan</a>, alder, <a href="/wiki/Maple" title="Maple">maple</a>, and fruit-tree woods are commonly used for smoking. Meat can also be cured by <a href="/wiki/Pickling" title="Pickling">pickling</a>, preserving in <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> or <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Salted_meat" class="mw-redirect" title="Salted meat">salted meat</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Curing_(food_preservation)" title="Curing (food preservation)">curing</a> methods). Other kinds of meat are <a href="/wiki/Marinade" class="mw-redirect" title="Marinade">marinated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbecue" title="Barbecue">barbecued</a>, or simply boiled, <a href="/wiki/Roasting" title="Roasting">roasted</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Frying" title="Frying">fried</a>.</p>
<p>Meat is generally eaten cooked, but many recipes call for raw beef, veal or fish (<a href="/wiki/Tartare" class="mw-redirect" title="Tartare">tartare</a>). <a href="/wiki/Steak_tartare" title="Steak tartare">Steak tartare</a> is a meat dish made from finely chopped or minced <a href="/wiki/Raw_food" class="mw-redirect" title="Raw food">raw</a> <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a> or <a href="/wiki/Horse_meat" title="Horse meat">horse meat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Meat is often spiced or seasoned, particularly with meat products such as sausages. Meat dishes are usually described by their source (animal and part of body) and method of preparation (e.g., a beef rib).</p>
<p>Meat is a typical base for making <a href="/wiki/Sandwich" title="Sandwich">sandwiches</a>. Popular varieties of sandwich meat include <a href="/wiki/Ham" title="Ham">ham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salami" title="Salami">salami</a> and other sausages, and <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Steak" title="Steak">steak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roast_beef" title="Roast beef">roast beef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corned_beef" title="Corned beef">corned beef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pepperoni" title="Pepperoni">pepperoni</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pastrami" title="Pastrami">pastrami</a>. Meat can also be molded or pressed (common for products that include offal, such as <a href="/wiki/Haggis" title="Haggis">haggis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scrapple" title="Scrapple">scrapple</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Canning" title="Canning">canned</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Red_meat#Potential_health_risks" title="Red meat">Health concerns associated with red meat</a></div>
<p>A study of 400,000 subjects conducted by the <a href="/wiki/European_Prospective_Investigation_into_Cancer_and_Nutrition" title="European Prospective Investigation into Cancer and Nutrition">European Prospective Investigation into Cancer and Nutrition</a> and published in 2013 showed "a moderate positive association between processed meat consumption and mortality, in particular due to cardiovascular diseases, but also to cancer."<sup id="cite_ref-BMC03713_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BMC03713-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 1999 <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">metastudy</a> combined data from five studies from western countries. The metastudy reported <a href="/wiki/Mortality_ratio" class="mw-redirect" title="Mortality ratio">mortality ratios</a>, where lower numbers indicated fewer deaths, for fish eaters to be 0.82, vegetarians to be 0.84, occasional meat eaters to be 0.84. Regular meat eaters and vegans shared the highest mortality ratio of 1.00.<sup id="cite_ref-AJCN_metastudy_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AJCN_metastudy-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response to changing prices as well as health concerns about saturated fat and cholesterol, consumers have altered their consumption of various meats. A USDA report points out that consumption of beef in the United States between 1970–1974 and 1990–1994 dropped by 21%, while consumption of <a href="/wiki/Chicken_(food)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicken (food)">chicken</a> increased by 90%.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> During the same period of time, the price of chicken dropped by 14% relative to the price of beef. In 1995 and 1996, beef consumption increased due to higher supplies and lower prices.</p>
<p>The <i>2015–2020 Dietary Guidelines for Americans</i> asked men and teenage boys to increase their consumption of vegetables or other underconsumed foods because they eat too much protein.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Various toxic compounds can contaminate meat, including <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mycotoxins" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycotoxins">mycotoxins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a> residues, and <a href="/wiki/Polyaromatic_hydrocarbons" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyaromatic hydrocarbons">polyaromatic hydrocarbons</a>. Often, these compounds are not very dangerous themselves but can be metabolized in the body to form harmful by-products, so any actual toxicological effects may depend on the individual genome, diet, and history of the consumer.<sup id="cite_ref-P.C3.BCssa_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P.C3.BCssa-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contamination">Contamination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Contamination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Meat and meat products may contain substances such as <a href="/wiki/Dioxin" title="Dioxin">dioxins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">polychlorinated biphenyl</a> (PCBs), and cooked meat may contain <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogens</a>, that are toxic to the consumer, although any chemical's toxicity is dependent on the dose and timing of exposure. Toxins may be introduced to meat as part of animal feed, as veterinary drug residues, or during processing and cooking.<sup id="cite_ref-P.C3.BCssa_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P.C3.BCssa-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Cancer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Carcinogenesis is the main long-term toxic response of consuming meat and meat byproducts.<sup id="cite_ref-P.C3.BCssa_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P.C3.BCssa-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Health concerns have been raised about the consumption of meat increasing the risk of cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-PLoS_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PLoS-63">[63]</a></sup> In particular, red meat and processed meat were found to be associated with higher risk of cancers of the <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung</a>, esophagus, liver, and <a href="/wiki/Colon_cancer" class="mw-redirect" title="Colon cancer">colon</a>, among others — although also a reduced risk for some minor type of cancers.<sup id="cite_ref-PLoS_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PLoS-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Agency_for_Research_on_Cancer" title="International Agency for Research on Cancer">International Agency for Research on Cancer</a> (IARC) is the specialized cancer agency of the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO). IARC classified processed meat (e.g., bacon, ham, hot dogs, sausages) as, "<i>carcinogenic to humans</i> (Group 1), based on <i>sufficient evidence</i> in humans that the consumption of processed meat causes colorectal cancer." IARC also classified red meat as "<i>probably carcinogenic to humans</i> (Group 2A), based on <i>limited evidence</i> that the consumption of red meat causes cancer in humans and <i>strong</i> mechanistic evidence supporting a carcinogenic effect."<sup id="cite_ref-WHO-20151026_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-20151026-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20151026_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151026-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC-20151026_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20151026-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another study found an increase risk of pancreatic cancer for red meat and pork. That study noted that, "findings suggest that intakes of red meat and processed meat are positively associated with pancreatic cancer risk and thus are potential target factors for disease prevention. [...] Future analyses of meat and pancreatic cancer risk should focus on meat preparation methods and related carcinogens." <sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>That study also suggests that fat and saturated fat are not likely contributors to pancreatic cancer. Animal fat, particularly from ruminants, tends to have a higher percentage of <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a> vs. monounsaturated and polyunsaturated fat when compared to vegetable fats, with the exception of some tropical plant fats;<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> consumption of which has been correlated with various health problems. The saturated fat found in meat has been associated with significantly raised risks of <a href="/wiki/Colon_cancer" class="mw-redirect" title="Colon cancer">colon cancer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> although evidence suggests that risks of prostate cancer are unrelated to animal fat consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other research does not support significant links between meat consumption and various cancers. Key et al. found that "There were no significant differences between vegetarians and nonvegetarians in mortality from cerebrovascular disease, stomach cancer, colorectal cancer, lung cancer, breast cancer, prostate cancer or all other causes combined."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Truswell reviewed numerous studies, concluding that the relationship of colorectal cancer with meat consumption appeared weaker than the "probable" status it had been given by the World Cancer Research Foundation in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> A study by Chao et al. (2005) found an apparent association of colorectal cancer with red meat consumption after adjustment for age and energy intake. However, after further adjustment for body mass index, cigarette smoking and other covariates, no association with red meat consumption was found.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Alex' ander conducted a meta-analysis which found no association of colorectal cancer with consumption of animal fat or protein.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Based on European data (EPIC-Oxford study), Key et al. found that incidence of colorectal cancer was somewhat lower among meat eaters than among vegetarians. However, they concluded that 'the study is not large enough to exclude small or moderate differences for specific causes of death, and more research on this topic is required'.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> A study within the European Prospective Investigation into Cancer and Nutrition (EPIC) found that association between esophageal cancer risk and total and processed meat intake was not statistically significant.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Another recent study of EPIC <sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> found a significant correlation between eating ' processed meat and cardiovascular diseases...also to cancer. In this population, reduction of processed meat consumption to less than 20&#160;g/day would prevent more than 3% of all deaths.'<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_disease">Heart disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Heart disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The correlation of consumption to increased risk of <a href="/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a> is controversial. Some studies fail to find a link between red meat consumption and heart disease<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> (although the same study found statistically significant correlation between the consumption of processed meat and coronary heart disease), while another study, a survey, conducted in 1960, of 25,153 California Seventh-Day Adventists, found that the risk of heart disease is three times greater for 45- to 64-year-old men who eat meat daily, versus those who did not eat meat.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> A major Harvard University study <sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> in 2010 involving over one million people who ate meat found that only processed meat had an adverse risk in relation to coronary heart disease. The study suggests that eating 50&#160;g (less than 2oz) of processed meat per day increases risk of coronary heart disease by 42%, and diabetes by 19%. Equivalent levels of fat, including saturated fats, in unprocessed meat (even when eating twice as much per day) did not show any deleterious effects, leading the researchers to suggest that "differences in salt and preservatives, rather than fats, might explain the higher risk of heart disease and diabetes seen with processed meats, but not with unprocessed red meats."</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Obesity">Obesity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Obesity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The EPIC-PANACEA study, published in 2010 in the <i>American Journal of Clinical Nutrition</i> closely tracked 373,803 people over a period of 8 years across 10 countries. It concluded that meat consumption is positively associated with weight gain in men and women.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Cattlemen%27s_Beef_Association" title="National Cattlemen's Beef Association">National Cattlemen's Beef Association</a> countered by stating that meat consumption may not be associated with fat gain.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In response, the authors of the original study controlled for just abdominal fat across a sample of 91,214 people and found that even when controlling for calories and lifestyle factors, meat consumption is linked with obesity.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Additional studies and reviews have confirmed the finding that greater meat consumption is positively linked with greater weight gain even when controlling for calories, and lifestyle factors.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacterial_contamination">Bacterial contamination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Bacterial contamination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2011 study by the <a href="/wiki/Translational_Genomics_Research_Institute" title="Translational Genomics Research Institute">Translational Genomics Research Institute</a> showed that nearly half (47%) of the meat and <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> in U.S. grocery stores were contaminated with <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">S. aureus</a></i>, with more than half (52%) of those bacteria resistant to antibiotics.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cooking">Cooking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Cooking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Meat can transmit certain <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">diseases</a>, but complete cooking and avoiding recontamination reduces this possibility.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several studies published since 1990 indicate that cooking muscle meat creates <a href="/wiki/Heterocyclic_amine" title="Heterocyclic amine">heterocyclic amines</a> (HCAs), which are thought to increase cancer risk in humans. Researchers at the National Cancer Institute published results of a study which found that human subjects who ate beef rare or medium-rare had less than one third the risk of stomach cancer than those who ate beef medium-well or well-done.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> While eating muscle meat raw may be the only way to avoid HCAs fully, the <a href="/wiki/National_Cancer_Institute" title="National Cancer Institute">National Cancer Institute</a> states that cooking meat below 212&#160;<a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">°F</a> (100&#160;<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a>) creates "negligible amounts" of HCAs. Also, <a href="/wiki/Microwaving" class="mw-redirect" title="Microwaving">microwaving</a> meat before cooking may reduce HCAs by 90%.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nitrosamine" title="Nitrosamine">Nitrosamines</a>, present in processed and cooked foods, have been noted as being carcinogenic, being linked to colon cancer. Also, toxic compounds called PAHs, or <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a>, present in processed, smoked and cooked foods, are known to be carcinogenic.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Meat_in_society">Meat in society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Meat in society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Meat is part of the human diet in most cultures, where it often has symbolic meaning and important social functions.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Many people, however, choose not to eat meat (this is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">vegetarianism</a>) or any food made from animals (<a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">veganism</a>). The reasons for not eating all or some meat may include ethical objections to killing animals for food, health concerns, environmental concerns or religious <a href="/wiki/Dietary_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Dietary laws">dietary laws</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics_of_eating_meat">Ethics of eating meat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Ethics of eating meat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_eating_meat" title="Ethics of eating meat">Ethics of eating meat</a></div>
<p>Ethical issues regarding the consumption of meat include objecting to the act of killing animals or to the <a href="/wiki/Intensive_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Intensive agriculture">agricultural practices</a> used in meat production. Reasons for objecting to killing animals for consumption may include <a href="/wiki/Animal_rights" title="Animal rights">animal rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_ethics" title="Environmental ethics">environmental ethics</a>, or an aversion to inflicting <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a> or harm on other <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentient creatures</a>. Some people, while not vegetarians, refuse to eat the flesh of certain animals (such as cows, pigs, cats, dogs, horses, or rabbits) due to cultural or religious traditions.</p>
<p>Some people eat only the flesh of animals that they believe have not been mistreated, and abstain from the flesh of animals raised in <a href="/wiki/Factory_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Factory farming">factory farms</a> or else abstain from particular products, such as <a href="/wiki/Foie_gras" title="Foie gras">foie gras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veal" title="Veal">veal</a>. Some people also abstain from milk and its derivatives for ethical reasons, because the production of veal is a byproduct of the <a href="/wiki/Dairy_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Dairy industry">dairy industry</a>. The ethical issues with intensive agriculture have to do with the concentration of animals, animal waste, and the potential for dead animals in a small space.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Some techniques of intensive agriculture may be cruel to animals: foie gras is a <a href="/wiki/Food_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Food product">food product</a> made from the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> of <a href="/wiki/Domestic_duck" title="Domestic duck">ducks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Domestic_goose" title="Domestic goose">geese</a> that have been <a href="/wiki/Force_feeding" class="mw-redirect" title="Force feeding">force fed</a> corn to fatten the organ; veal is criticised because the veal calves may be highly restricted in movement, have unsuitable flooring, spend their entire lives indoors, experience prolonged deprivation (sensory, social, and exploratory), and be more susceptible to high amounts of stress and disease.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_traditions">Religious traditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Religious traditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism_and_religion" title="Vegetarianism and religion">Vegetarianism and religion</a></div>
<p>The religion of <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> has always opposed eating meat, and there are also <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_vegetarianism" title="Buddhist vegetarianism">schools of Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism#Vegetarian_Hindus" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> that condemn the eating of meat. <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> dietary rules (<i><a href="/wiki/Kashrut" title="Kashrut">Kashrut</a></i>) allow certain (<i><a href="/wiki/Kosher" class="mw-redirect" title="Kosher">kosher</a></i>) meat and forbid other (<i><a href="/wiki/Treif" title="Treif">treif</a></i>). The rules include prohibitions on the consumption of <a href="/wiki/Unclean_animals" class="mw-redirect" title="Unclean animals">unclean animals</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a> including <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">mollusca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crustacea" class="mw-redirect" title="Crustacea">crustacea</a>, and most <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>), and mixtures of meat and milk. Similar rules apply in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_dietary_laws" title="Islamic dietary laws">Islamic dietary laws</a>: The <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> explicitly forbids meat from animals that die naturally, blood, the meat of swine (porcine animals, pigs), and animals dedicated to other than Allah (either undedicated or dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idols</a>) which are <a href="/wiki/Haram" title="Haram">haram</a> as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> forbids meat of slowly slaughtered animals ("<a href="/wiki/Kutha_meat" title="Kutha meat">kutha</a>") and prescribes killing animals with a single strike ("<a href="/wiki/Jhatka" title="Jhatka">jhatka</a>"), but some Sikh groups oppose eating any meat.<sup id="cite_ref-Takhar2005_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Takhar2005-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Psychology_of_eating_meat" title="Psychology of eating meat">Psychology of eating meat</a></div>
<p>Research in <a href="/wiki/Applied_psychology" title="Applied psychology">applied psychology</a> has investigated practices of meat eating in relation to <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Personality" title="Personality">personality</a> characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-Loughnan2014_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Loughnan2014-95">[95]</a></sup> Psychological research suggests meat eating is correlated with <a href="/wiki/Masculinity" title="Masculinity">masculinity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Rozin2012_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rozin2012-96">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Social_dominance_orientation" title="Social dominance orientation">support for social hierarchy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> and reduced <a href="/wiki/Big_Five_personality_traits#Openness_to_experience" title="Big Five personality traits">openness to experience</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Keller2015_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keller2015-98">[98]</a></sup> Research into the <a href="/wiki/Consumer_psychology" class="mw-redirect" title="Consumer psychology">consumer psychology</a> of meat is relevant both to <a href="/wiki/Meat_industry" title="Meat industry">meat industry</a> <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">marketing</a><sup id="cite_ref-Richardson1994_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richardson1994-99">[99]</a></sup> and to advocates of reduced meat consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Kl.C3.B6ckner_2014_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kl.C3.B6ckner_2014-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sch.C3.B6sler_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sch.C3.B6sler-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
